  //<![CDATA[
    function loadScript(url, callback) {
      var script = document.createElement("script");
      script.type = "text/javascript";
    
      if (script.readyState) {  //Internet Explorer
          script.onreadystatechange = function() {
            if (script.readyState == "loaded" ||
                    script.readyState == "complete") {
              script.onreadystatechange = null;
              callback();
            }
          };
      } else {  //Other browsers
        script.onload = function() {
          callback();
        };
      }
    
      script.src = url;
      document.getElementsByTagName("head")[0].appendChild(script);
    }
    
    function initAppleId() {
      AppleID.auth.init({
        clientId : 'com.goodreads.app', 
        scope : 'name email',
        redirectURI: 'https://www.goodreads.com/apple_users/sign_in_with_apple_web',
        state: 'apple_oauth_state_6864ab97-8fc2-4626-b41b-fb89151eae61'
      });
    }
    
    var initializeSiwa = function() {
      var APPLE_SIGN_IN_JS_URL =  "https://appleid.cdn-apple.com/appleauth/static/jsapi/appleid/1/en_US/appleid.auth.js"
      loadScript(APPLE_SIGN_IN_JS_URL, initAppleId);
    };

      initAppleId();

      initializeSiwa();

<span id="freeTextContainer13430812271766733646">A stranger rode out of the heart of the great glowing West, into the small Wyoming valley in the summer of 1889.<br/> It was Shane, who appeared on the horizon and became a friend and guardian to the Starrett family at a time when homesteaders and cattle rangers battled for territory and survival.   <span id="freeText13430812271766733646" style="display:none">A stranger rode out of the heart of the great glowing West, into the small Wyoming valley in the summer of 1889.<br /> It was Shane, who appeared on the horizon and became a friend and guardian to the Starrett family at a time when homesteaders and cattle rangers battled for territory and survival. Jack Schaefer’s classic novel illuminates the spirit of the West through the eyes of a young boy and a hero who changes the lives of everyone around him. Renowned artist Wendell Minor provides stunning images and a moving introduction to this new edition of Shane, the ultimate tale of the Western landscape.</span>

<div class='answererUserIcon'>
<a class="leftAlignedImage userPhoto" href="/user/show/64258130-rick"><img alt="" src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/users/1485374175i/64258130._UX100_CR0,0,100,100_.jpg" /></a>
</div>
<div class='communityAnswerTextContainer'>
<span class='communityAnswererName'>
<a href="/user/show/64258130-rick">Rick</a>
</span>
<span class='communityAnswerText'>
<div class='spoiler'>
This answer contains spoilers…
<span class='spoilerAnswerText'>
<a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[
  
    Although Joe and Marian knew the true nature of Shane's past, Bob did not. In Bob's eyes, Shane had become a figure of admiration that surpassed even <a href="#" rel="nofollow noopener">…more</a>
    Although Joe and Marian knew the true nature of Shane's past, Bob did not. In Bob's eyes, Shane had become a figure of admiration that surpassed even his father. As long as Bob was unaware of Shane's true nature Shane could "live" with it. After the killing however, Shane could no longer stomach being and idol for this boy that he loved so dearly, with a clear conscience. He couldn't imagine this boy growing up straight and strong while idolizing a killer. He had to leave.<br /><br />The other answers are incorrect: <br />He wasn't afraid being a burden; the "Shane" of this novel could never die. Although he loved her almost as much as he loved Bob, Marian was never his "girlfriend" in any sense of the word and he helped the family for the love of all three but equally, because it was the right thing to do.<a href="#" rel="nofollow noopener">(less)</a>
  
 <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span>
</span>
</div>
</span>

<span id="freeTextContainer991199439205215950">Fantastic storytelling. A great classic Western. All the elements needed:<br/><br/>- A dark and mysterious stranger<br/>- A passionate narrator<br/>- The oppression of the little people<br/>- The evil cattle Baron <br/>- Gunslingers<br/>- Clever one liners<br/>- Riding off into the sunset <br/><br/>Even if you don't like westerns, if you enjoy a good, well-written story I think you will enjoy this one. (also, it isn't very long so it is a nice quick read)</span>

          </span>

<div id="review_374394002" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/374394002" />
    <a title="Checkman" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5117003-checkman"><img alt="Checkman" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1591798821p2/5117003.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/374394002?book_show_action=true">Jul 22, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Checkman" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Checkman" href="/user/show/5117003-checkman">Checkman</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Mass Market Paperback 9780553240764" href="/book/show/2766994-shane">review of another edition</a>

          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">Fans of great stories</span></div>

        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5117003-checkman?shelf=western">western</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5117003-checkman?shelf=beach-read">beach-read</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer374394002" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1488576610227009367">A classic that is more than a western. It's also a story about one of the myths of the United States - the heroic Lone Hero on the Frontier. Shane is the embodiment of the Lone Hero; the man who shares the values of civilized society, but has the destructive skills of the outlaw.He rides out of the wilderness to aid the band of pioneers and take on the land-grabbing cattle barons in a violent but satisfying bloodletting. In 2012 this is a cliche, but ,as I have pointed out in the past when revie</span>
  <span id="freeText1488576610227009367" style="display:none">A classic that is more than a western. It's also a story about one of the myths of the United States - the heroic Lone Hero on the Frontier. Shane is the embodiment of the Lone Hero; the man who shares the values of civilized society, but has the destructive skills of the outlaw.He rides out of the wilderness to aid the band of pioneers and take on the land-grabbing cattle barons in a violent but satisfying bloodletting. In 2012 this is a cliche, but ,as I have pointed out in the past when reviewing older stories, <i>Shane</i> is the archetype. It still has a freshness because it's one of the first of it's type.<br /><br />In reality such men never existed. The real world is more messy. Shane is a mythical individual, but what makes Shane (the character) work so well is that <i>Shane</i> is told from the perspective of a young boy, or at least the memories of a young boy.The narrator is an adult, but he doesn't clutter the account with adult sensibilities. The man Shane is mythical because that is how children see the adults that they admire. There is no cynicism in Shaffer's story. It's an honest and heartfelt telling because that is how we view the world when we are young. <br /><br /> Now there are times the writing does verge on being pulpy such as when Shane is confronting the gunfighter brought in by the cattle baron. Here is one small sample.<br /><br />“I’m waiting, Wilson. Do I have to crowd you into slapping leather?”<br /><br />Nevertheless it's very good pulp and even though it was originally sold to <i>Argosy</i> magazine as a serial the story rose above it's origins - which is why it is a classic. Both the novel and the movie. <br /><br />Good......no make that a great story. Even if you don't like westerns try <i>Shane</i>. You won't be sorry.</span>
  <a data-text-id="1488576610227009367" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1902904307" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1902904307" />
    <a title="Edward Lorn" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/8850418-edward-lorn"><img alt="Edward Lorn" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1492199506p2/8850418.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1902904307?book_show_action=true">Feb 04, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Edward Lorn" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Edward Lorn" href="/user/show/8850418-edward-lorn">Edward Lorn</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Mass Market Paperback 9780553240764" href="/book/show/2766994-shane">review of another edition</a>

          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">Everyone</span></div>

        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1902904307" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3356423937443288886"><a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/257837.Shane' title='Shane by Jack Schaefer' rel='nofollow noopener'>Shane</a> was my father's favorite movie. I remember watching it with him on many occasions. The film remains one of the only positive memories I have of my old man. I was never a fan of westerns, but as a kid who desperately wanted his father's approval (approval that, I might add, would never come; he's been dead since 2011), I'd force myself to consume the things he liked in the hopes that I might become one of those things.<br/><br/>And then I grew up and realized my father wasn't someone whose approval I</span>
  <span id="freeText3356423937443288886" style="display:none"><a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/257837.Shane" title="Shane by Jack Schaefer" rel="nofollow noopener">Shane</a> was my father's favorite movie. I remember watching it with him on many occasions. The film remains one of the only positive memories I have of my old man. I was never a fan of westerns, but as a kid who desperately wanted his father's approval (approval that, I might add, would never come; he's been dead since 2011), I'd force myself to consume the things he liked in the hopes that I might become one of those things.<br /><br />And then I grew up and realized my father wasn't someone whose approval I should be concerned with. Still, the film adaptation of this book remains one of my favorite movies, if for no other reason than that it was something I shared with my father. The book itself is terrific and gets my highest recommendation.<br /><br />One final note, an inside look, if you will... People assume that when I say negative reviews don't bother me that my words are nothing but posturing. "Of course he cares about reviews. What author doesn't?" But I mean what I say. And I can thank my father for that. If you hate me and/or my work, that doesn't affect me in the slightest. I find some reasoning silly and others thought-provoking, but I never take reviews to heart because they are opinion not truth. I had to teach myself at an early age that truth and opinion are two different things. I had to for my own mental survival. There I was, winning writing contests and spelling bees and science fairs, and my father took every one of my successes as a slight against him. "Oh, you think you're hot shit because you can read? Well you ain't ever gonna be shit beyond that, so enjoy your fifteen minutes." That's a direct quote from Dad. Classy motherfucker, huh? So what the fuck do I care if you hate me? My own father didn't like me and I turned out just fine. But keep on giving up that free real estate to me in your mind. I'll be over here paying the bills.<br /><br />In summation: This was my first time reading this novella and it certainly will not be my last. It's just long enough. Any more of it would have been to overstay its welcome. I might be a bit biased, though, seeing as how the book speaks to me on a personal level, but I dug it. The writing is fantastic, as well. This is so much more than a dime-store western and it deserves your attention.<br /><br /><b>Final Judgment: Required reading.</b></span>
  <a data-text-id="3356423937443288886" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2904297337?book_show_action=true">Jul 20, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Charles  van Buren" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Charles  van Buren" href="/user/show/47208326-charles-van-buren">Charles  van Buren</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Kindle Edition " href="/book/show/35163503-shane">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2904297337" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3174164483437132507">A deceptively simple tale<br/><br/>Review of Kindle edition<br/>Publication date: May 14, 2017<br/>Publisher: University of New Mexico Press<br/>Language: English<br/>ASIN: B0725P6KR2<br/><br/>A great piece of American literature which happens to be a western but is much more than just a well written western story. Even if you have seen the 1953 released movie with Alan Ladd, and maybe even more so if you have, the tension builds as you read until the explosive, but expected, climax is reached. <br/><br/>Shane is a man attempting to escape the </span>
  <span id="freeText3174164483437132507" style="display:none">A deceptively simple tale<br /><br />Review of Kindle edition<br />Publication date: May 14, 2017<br />Publisher: University of New Mexico Press<br />Language: English<br />ASIN: B0725P6KR2<br /><br />A great piece of American literature which happens to be a western but is much more than just a well written western story. Even if you have seen the 1953 released movie with Alan Ladd, and maybe even more so if you have, the tension builds as you read until the explosive, but expected, climax is reached. <br /><br />Shane is a man attempting to escape the past but it can't be done. He can't escape himself. He tells the boy, Bob Starrett, "A man is what he is, Bob, and there’s no breaking the mold. I tried that and I’ve lost.". But even as he lost, he won on another level.<br /><br /> If you are looking for a Louis L'Amour style western, this isn't it. Jack Schaefer's first novel is an easy to read but powerful, moving novel, not just another western adventure tale.<br /><br />Note: despite what you may see on Goodreads, this is not the critical edition of Shane. Nor, so far as I can tell, is it the pre-1954 text. In 1954 SHANE was supposedly edited to remove offensive words. The only thing I can find in this edition which could be considered even remotely offensive is this passage:<br /><br />"You’re a damn fool, Wright. But what can you expect from a breed?” <br />“That’s a lie!” shouted Ernie. “My mother wasn’t no Indian!” <br />“Why, you crossbred squatter,” Wilson said, quick and sharp, “are you telling me I’m wrong?” <br />“I’m telling you you’re a God-damned liar!”<br /><br />In this passage, Wilson was being deliberately offensive and insulting to goad Ernie into a gunfight. I really don't see how a reader could be offended unless the reader is as hot headed as Ernie. Anyway, this seems to be the edited text.</span>
  <a data-text-id="3174164483437132507" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1848501659" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1848501659" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer8493240636741252291">Good book. This is another where the movie will probably forever more be better known than the novel itself. In this case it's also a good movie..it happens now and then you know. As they say even a blind hog finds an acorn occasionally.<br/><br/>Anyway...<a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/257837.Shane' title='Shane by Jack Schaefer' rel='nofollow noopener'>Shane</a>. The book opens with the (almost stereotypical) dark rider riding through and meeting a family whom he befriends. But if you think about it...this is one or the characters that established the stereotype. So he (Shane) becomes almost a part of said</span>
  <span id="freeText8493240636741252291" style="display:none">Good book. This is another where the movie will probably forever more be better known than the novel itself. In this case it's also a good movie..it happens now and then you know. As they say even a blind hog finds an acorn occasionally.<br /><br />Anyway...<a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/257837.Shane" title="Shane by Jack Schaefer" rel="nofollow noopener">Shane</a>. The book opens with the (almost stereotypical) dark rider riding through and meeting a family whom he befriends. But if you think about it...this is one or the characters that established the stereotype. So he (Shane) becomes almost a part of said family. This novel rests strongly on some very well done character development. You will get to know these people.<br /><br />I can recommend this one to everybody. You know there are Westerns that happen to be good books then there are good books that happen to be Westerns. This is the latter.<br /><br />Enjoy. Recommended.</span>

<div id="review_385939172" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/385939172" />
    <a title="Gary" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2190708-gary"><img alt="Gary" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1556466519p2/2190708.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/385939172?book_show_action=true">Aug 07, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Gary" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Gary" href="/user/show/2190708-gary">Gary</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780553271102" href="/book/show/1072364.Shane">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer385939172" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11226087584769162110">I read this book in high school in Sophomore English class. The teacher I had was wonderful,and she was out 8 weeks due to surgery. Thank God she was with us when we read,and discussed this book. I don't really remember the discussion in class much, but I did remember that I loved this book when I was a kid.....then after reading it the first time in 1976....I wondered why she had it on our class roster of novels to read.......so...here I am 2012,and I downloaded it onto my NOOK. I was prepared </span>
  <span id="freeText11226087584769162110" style="display:none">I read this book in high school in Sophomore English class. The teacher I had was wonderful,and she was out 8 weeks due to surgery. Thank God she was with us when we read,and discussed this book. I don't really remember the discussion in class much, but I did remember that I loved this book when I was a kid.....then after reading it the first time in 1976....I wondered why she had it on our class roster of novels to read.......so...here I am 2012,and I downloaded it onto my NOOK. I was prepared to be disappointed reading it again after all these years,and being so much older, and wiser....supposedly......<br /><br />Well, after finishing it last night the verdict is in.....I loved it again....I truly did.<br /><br />If you are not a Western novel fan....read this one anyway....it's about fighting for what you truly believe in at all costs,and defending your position on what you think is right.....not spoiling it for anyone....Shane is a role model,and a good one. A good one for young people, like Bob , the young boy in the novel....I think the teacher had us read this novel for that point..... that Shane had some things right in his way of life,and we needed to experience that. I applaud the author for a truly inspiring book for young people with a positive role model...to fight,and fight hard for that you think is right, not worrying about the ramifications of what others think about you, or what you believe in....this novel hits that nail right on the head.....<br /><br />I highly recommend this one. Teachers out there, use this with your students, it has a truly inspiring message,and maybe a message for us wee adults to be reminded of what's the right thing to to do,and what it means to be a "man" or a "woman" due to unsavory situations and people you have to deal with......This novel sends the message that you have to be strong and determined to truly cope with what life throws at you....Shane is truly my hero,and it made me weep to finish those last words on the last page, before bedtime.<br /><br />Folks, the verdict is in......read this one....truly read this one,and enjoy!<br /><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="11226087584769162110" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_3425527306" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3425527306" />
    <a title="robin friedman" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/22269025-robin-friedman"><img alt="robin friedman" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1571353109p2/22269025.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3425527306?book_show_action=true">Jul 04, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="robin friedman" class="user" itemprop="url" name="robin friedman" href="/user/show/22269025-robin-friedman">robin friedman</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3425527306" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer2569878692820791051">Shane For Independence Day<br/><br/>Each year for the Fourth of July, I try to review a book that captures something of the spirit of the day and of our country. This year, I chose Jack Schaefer's 1949 book, "Shane" his first novel and the source of the famous 1953 film of the same name, directed by George Stevens, starring Alan Ladd, and with a screenplay by the Western novelist, A.B. Guthrie. I chose this book for Independence Day because of the sense of promise it shows for the United States and becaus</span>
  <span id="freeText2569878692820791051" style="display:none">Shane For Independence Day<br /><br />Each year for the Fourth of July, I try to review a book that captures something of the spirit of the day and of our country. This year, I chose Jack Schaefer's 1949 book, "Shane" his first novel and the source of the famous 1953 film of the same name, directed by George Stevens, starring Alan Ladd, and with a screenplay by the Western novelist, A.B. Guthrie. I chose this book for Independence Day because of the sense of promise it shows for the United States and because of the book's sense of vision and myth-making, qualities which our country sorely needs in difficult times.<br /><br />Schaefer's novel is set in Wyoming territory in 1889 and is narrated in the first person by Bob Starrett who at the time of the story he tells as an adult was eleven years old. At the outset of the story, Bob is enamored by a mysterious, well-dressed stranger who rides from a distance to the small family farm of his parents, Joe and Marian Starrett. The family are homesteaders trying to establish themselves in a new life. Joe had been a cowboy and the New England born Marian had been a school teacher.<br /><br />"Call me Shane" the lean, tough stranger says as he and the Starretts become acquainted. Shane's past remains mysterious but troubled. He doesn't wear a gun but clearly is not a man to be trifled with. The Starretts and Shane soon establish a bond, with Shane staying on the farm to work as a hired hand while he gazes longingly in the distance at the mountains.<br /><br />The film shows conflict and change among three ways of life: the lonely, romantic wanderer and gunfighter represented by Shane, the rising life of the Western small farm and town of the Starrett's and the life of the rancher, represented by Luke Fletcher and his minions. Fletcher is at war with the farmers who use water and land he needs to graze cattle. When Shane comes into the story he becomes attached to the Starretts and their integrity and search for independence. He becomes fond of young Bob who idolizes the charismatic Shane in return. Shane and Joe Starrett form a bond of mutual respect. Shane and Marian become deeply attracted to each other in a way that is painfully obvious to all.<br /><br />The story involves a fight between Starrett and Shane on the one hand and Fletcher and his hired gun, Stark Wilson, on the other hand. Beginning slowly on the Starrett farm, the novel works to a tense climax and a sharp gunfight in which Shane emerges victorious but severely, likely fatally, wounded. Shane rides off alone and wounded into the mountains telling Bob "Go home to your mother and father. Grow strong and straight and take care of them. Both of them."<br /><br />The novel is short with simple, beautiful writing that would be the envy of many more self-consciously literary book. The romantic gunfighter Shane comes to usher in a new more settled way of life in the West. The change comes with its costs. Shane and his mystery becomes a symbol for the romance of the American way of life while the Starrett's and their fellow homesteaders show an idealized version of settlement, education, and the value of hard work. Bob carries the vision of his youthful encounter with Shane with him throughout his life, and so, in the aim of the novel, should the reader.<br /><br />Many people who have seen the 1953 film are probably unfamiliar with Schaefer's novel. At one time, the book appeared on many high school reading lists. The film follows the book reasonably closely, but reading the book is a treasure of its own. "Shane" is included in an upcoming Library of America volume "The Western: Four Classic Novels of the 1940s and 50s" edited by Ron Hansen as well as in a separate paperback edition. It is an outstanding American novel to get to know and a book which will encourage visionary reflection on the United States and its promise on this Independence Day.<br /><br />Robin Friedman</span>
  <a data-text-id="2569878692820791051" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2256400580" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2256400580" />
    <a title="Conor" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/30552336-conor"><img alt="Conor" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1400278909p2/30552336.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2256400580?book_show_action=true">Jan 13, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Conor" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Conor" href="/user/show/30552336-conor">Conor</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2256400580" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17746441435053888297"><i> There's no living with a killing. There's no going back from it. Right or wrong, it's a brand, a brand that sticks. There's no going back. Now you run on home to your mother and tell her, tell her everything's alright, and there aren't any more guns in the valley. </i> <br/><br/>Watched Shane for the 3rd or 4th time over the Christmas (the first time I've seen it since seeing Logan over the summer which referenced extensively) so I thought I'd drop one of my iconic not-read-the-book reviews. <br/><br/>Shane is best re</span>
  <span id="freeText17746441435053888297" style="display:none"><i> There's no living with a killing. There's no going back from it. Right or wrong, it's a brand, a brand that sticks. There's no going back. Now you run on home to your mother and tell her, tell her everything's alright, and there aren't any more guns in the valley. </i> <br /><br />Watched Shane for the 3rd or 4th time over the Christmas (the first time I've seen it since seeing Logan over the summer which referenced extensively) so I thought I'd drop one of my iconic not-read-the-book reviews. <br /><br />Shane is best remembered (by me at least) as a film with great scenes (the opening scene, the bar-fight, the final scene) but rewatching it I was struck by how well those scenes were tied together by everything else in between. The cinematography is beautiful, the characters are well-drawn and acted and the tone and feeling throughout the film is great.<br /><br />One of my favourite films from last year, Logan, referenced Shane quite a bit and it's easy to draw parallels between them (especially as Shane is one of the great Western archetypes and Logan was imo more a Western than a Superhero film). While it may be unfair to Logan to criticize it for falling short of an all-time great film (and I'd like to state again that Logan was a great film that does lots of brilliant stuff) there was one area I felt that Logan really suffered in the comparison and that's as a study of violence. <br /><br />Logan made an effort to examine the psychological impact of violence (primarily in how it haunts the Professor and Logan. This, which is a great scene, springs to mind: <a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=plvBEhg4EsY">https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=plvBE...</a>) which is admirable but this effort was rather undermined by the constant action-movie violence especially when you have a 12 year old girl somersaulting over people and decapitating them with metal claws. Shane also deals with the psychological and philosophical consequences of violence but the more restrained violence in the film allows it to be done much more skillfully. In Shane, which has less violence than would be allowed in any modern action-oriented film the sparing use of violence makes each encounter feel truly meaningful and lends weight to the idea, without it even having to be said, that violence, and especially killing, is a serious and irreversible matter.<br /><br />Overall Shane's iconic scenes, sympathetic characters, beautifully shot cinematography and deftly handled plot make it a timeless classic. Even after more than 60 years and several viewings it's still one of my favourite films.</span>
  <a data-text-id="17746441435053888297" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    <a title="Cphe" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/14907696-cphe"><img alt="Cphe" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1378006896p2/14907696.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1860909280?book_show_action=true">Jan 18, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Cphe" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Cphe" href="/user/show/14907696-cphe">Cphe</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Kindle Edition " href="/book/show/29343669-shane">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:

<div id="review_963825621" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/963825621" />
    <a title="Jeremiah Boydstun" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/20701116-jeremiah-boydstun"><img alt="Jeremiah Boydstun" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1373256129p2/20701116.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/963825621?book_show_action=true">Jun 10, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Jeremiah Boydstun" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Jeremiah Boydstun" href="/user/show/20701116-jeremiah-boydstun">Jeremiah Boydstun</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/20701116-jeremiah-boydstun?shelf=westerns">westerns</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer963825621" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer5673003604040921641">Contains minor spoilers<br/><br/>Loved this book from beginning to end for two primary reasons:<br/><br/>First, Schaefer has an elegant and simple style that is, much like the story itself, quintessentially American: relatively short declarative sentences, the sparing and precise use of modifiers, and the brief and intense focus on those aspects of characterization and setting that highlight common themes and images in American fiction (but themes that are also timeless and universal). Some of these themes include </span>
  <span id="freeText5673003604040921641" style="display:none">Contains minor spoilers<br /><br />Loved this book from beginning to end for two primary reasons:<br /><br />First, Schaefer has an elegant and simple style that is, much like the story itself, quintessentially American: relatively short declarative sentences, the sparing and precise use of modifiers, and the brief and intense focus on those aspects of characterization and setting that highlight common themes and images in American fiction (but themes that are also timeless and universal). Some of these themes include man versus nature, the lone gunslinger with a checkered and mysterious past, the abuse of power and the power to control that abuse, coming of age, and pride. I found myself continually amazed at Schaefer’s ability to provide such vivid descriptions while using so little language. I was also impressed with his ability to efficiently build a story around some rather profound philosophical and ethical issues without diminishing the import of those issues by editorializing or having the characters ruminate on them; the issues are presented in plain fashion and the reader is left to determine their weight and relevance based on the facts of the story. <br /><br />Furthermore, Shaefer is very deliberate and precise with his pacing, his organization and his structuring of the story, so that once, for example, the issue of justifiable violence is introduced, it is always present, even when it is not written about or spoken of by the characters. In brief, it’s amazing how much content Shaefer packs into such a short story. <br /><br />Second, this story is noteworthy for what Shaefer doesn’t pack into it. As a writer, Shaefer understands what a story needs to be successful: an interesting storyline, an interesting set of characters, and an interesting stage upon which everything will be acted out. As for the storyline, there is so much that is hidden from the reader, but this is what makes the story so savory. From beginning to end, the mystery of Shane is preserved. He is and remains an enigma, a cypher. In many ways, he comes to embody the mythos of the old West, a place that is both beautiful and dangerous, a place whose origins are contested and its future unclear (something also represented by the feud between the farmers, a group attempting to lay down roots, and Fletcher, the itinerant cattle baron). <br /><br />The true nature of the relationship between Shane and Marion is also portrayed in an ambivalent fashion. Much is said between them but even more is said in the looks and in the silences that pass between these two characters. Similarly, much of the real dialogue between Shane and Joe remains unspoken. <br /><br />Fletcher also remains the reclusive villain, only making a brief appearance at the end, and the true motives of why the characters do what they do (why, for example, is Wilson such a cold-blooded killer; why does Chris have a change of heart at the end?) remains vague. This makes the characters interesting because they remain individuals with private lives that readers are denied access to; it keeps the characters human and preserves their dignity, the latter of which is an important theme in the novel. <br /><br />To conclude, this is a wonderful novel that moves at a brisk pace, that is filled with drama and expectation, and that is superbly written. A book that everyone should read at some point. <br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="5673003604040921641" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2277043278" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2277043278" />
    <a title="William Johnson" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2820455-william-johnson"><img alt="William Johnson" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1442598729p2/2820455.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2277043278?book_show_action=true">Feb 15, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="William Johnson" class="user" itemprop="url" name="William Johnson" href="/user/show/2820455-william-johnson">William Johnson</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Mass Market Paperback " href="/book/show/31210594-shane">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2820455-william-johnson?shelf=westerns-1940s">westerns-1940s</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2820455-william-johnson?shelf=since-joining-goodreads">since-joining-goodreads</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2820455-william-johnson?shelf=mysterious-stranger">mysterious-stranger</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2820455-william-johnson?shelf=2018">2018</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2277043278" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13830124452776231949">My first Western! And what a way to start ... <br/><br/>I suddenly was compelled to read and watch Westerns, something I had only brief knowledge or experience in. I'm not sure why, exactly, the genre left me behind because I grew up watching Star Trek which is essentially, as Gene Roddenberry explained, "Wagon Train to the Stars". A lot of the same concepts and ideas in Trek apply to Western tropes as well. So I had some of the ideas ingrained in me but just never took the official plunge into the subjec</span>
  <span id="freeText13830124452776231949" style="display:none">My first Western! And what a way to start ... <br /><br />I suddenly was compelled to read and watch Westerns, something I had only brief knowledge or experience in. I'm not sure why, exactly, the genre left me behind because I grew up watching Star Trek which is essentially, as Gene Roddenberry explained, "Wagon Train to the Stars". A lot of the same concepts and ideas in Trek apply to Western tropes as well. So I had some of the ideas ingrained in me but just never took the official plunge into the subject matter proper.<br /><br />So almost everyone I trust advised me to go with Shane as a starter book and I can see why. It doesn't challenge the genre, exactly, and comfortably gets you involved with the stereotypical moments you'd find in a Western. But, like The Godfather did for mob films, Shane might hit familiar beats to the modern reader but also happens to DEFINE the genre. You might be familiar with the goings-on but a true STORY, if compelling and honest, will ALWAYS deliver.<br /><br />So this book came as both a comfortable introduction to the Western genre as well as a comfort-food, which seems paradoxical, I know, but ... it rings true.<br /><br />I, of course, read some reviews of the books. I got this next quotation from Wikipedia (where factual mileage may vary) but I found it compelling:<br /><br />"This was Jack Schaefer’s first novel. He preferred in later years to write stories less mythic and more attuned to the real West.... Although he is little known, and the volume of his work is small, he surely ranks as one of this nation’s greatest." -- Richard S Wheeler<br /><br />I've noticed that in the movie world, since Unforgiven mostly, Westerns have let go of the mythic quality and approached the gritty realism and violence of the frontier. Shane seemed to embrace both: it has a mythic quality (the undefeatable, "perfect" stranger) but also introduces ignorant readers like me to the basics of frontier society. Sometimes it is good to have that mixture. It is entertainment after all.<br /><br />I didn't really touch on the manliness of this book. This book is aggressively male but, surprisingly, not in a negative or out-of-touch way. Going back to Star Trek, this story focuses on morals and a code of honor that just happens to be singularly attributed to males (the book was written in the 1940s after all). There are scenes of men viewing other men in their honorable glory getting teared up in pride. With a lesser author, it could be comical. It comes off as honest here.<br /><br />So there you have it. My first Western! And I will definitely be coming back for more!</span>
  <a data-text-id="13830124452776231949" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <span id="freeText13207023771396245828" style="display:none">What a fantastic story! <div id="review_834806" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/834806" />
    <a title="ward" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/66596-ward"><img alt="ward" src="https://s.gr-assets.com/assets/nophoto/user/m_50x66-82093808bca726cb3249a493fbd3bd0f.png" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/834806?book_show_action=true">Apr 22, 2007</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="ward" class="user" itemprop="url" name="ward" href="/user/show/66596-ward">ward</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780803291423" href="/book/show/455851.Shane">review of another edition</a>

          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">Anyone</span></div>

        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer834806" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11133311731034310715">This is the book I grew up with, having it read to me as a boy. It is an American classic and considered one of the great Westerns in league only with Lonesome Dove.<br/><br/>This is THE novel from which the wester movie genre was created. The dark hero with a mixed and unstated past, the western town with a struggle raging between migrant farmers and cattle ranchers, the hired guns and dark saloons all comprise elements of this short story. This is THE story that gave birth to the image of the laconic co</span>
  <span id="freeText11133311731034310715" style="display:none">This is the book I grew up with, having it read to me as a boy. It is an American classic and considered one of the great Westerns in league only with Lonesome Dove.<br /><br />This is THE novel from which the wester movie genre was created. The dark hero with a mixed and unstated past, the western town with a struggle raging between migrant farmers and cattle ranchers, the hired guns and dark saloons all comprise elements of this short story. This is THE story that gave birth to the image of the laconic cowboy - but does so by revealing adulthood through the eyes of the narrator, a young boy who wants to be a man and is torn between idols - the grace of his gentle but strong father and the violence and danger of an honrable but displaced drifter.<br /><br />The first few pages alone are one vivid and unforgetable description of Shane, an unknown cowboy as he rides up a long road in the summer sun to the shade of a tree.<br /><br />This is the book that taught a generation of American men, what it was to be strong and proud - and could only have been written by an American in America. It must be understood that what today may appear cliche is in fact the original gold standard from which the cliche poorly copied.<br /><br />I recommend this book to everyone, boys, girls, men and women. Moreover I recommend this edition. The original story being roughly 150 pages, this edition provides twice that in contextual information concerning this stories creation, its impact on American culture and the idealistic image of an american culture it represents.<br /><br />This book is a must read.</span>
  <a data-text-id="11133311731034310715" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer10938405921445528283">I'm not a fan of Westerns as a genre, yet I loved this book back in 1968 when I first read it as a sophomore in high school. Since then, I've seen the movie multiple times, and it was time to re-read the book (especially after I recommended it to a young man who isn't a big reader). As an adult, I was drawn to the descriptions of Shane as he wrestles with his demons and angels. I still love the book, although now, in my mind's eye, I see Van Heflin as Joe Starrett, Jean Arthur as Marian, Brandon</span>
  <span id="freeText10938405921445528283" style="display:none">I'm not a fan of Westerns as a genre, yet I loved this book back in 1968 when I first read it as a sophomore in high school. Since then, I've seen the movie multiple times, and it was time to re-read the book (especially after I recommended it to a young man who isn't a big reader). As an adult, I was drawn to the descriptions of Shane as he wrestles with his demons and angels. I still love the book, although now, in my mind's eye, I see Van Heflin as Joe Starrett, Jean Arthur as Marian, Brandon DeWilde as Bob (named Joey in the movie), and, of course, Alan Ladd as Shane.</span>

<div id="review_278566115" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/278566115" />
    <a title="Ms. Yingling" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2636606-ms-yingling"><img alt="Ms. Yingling" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1376174508p2/2636606.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/278566115?book_show_action=true">Feb 16, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Ms. Yingling" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Ms. Yingling" href="/user/show/2636606-ms-yingling">Ms. Yingling</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer278566115" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3827886451365485783">Not a fan of westerns, never seen the movie with Alan Ladd, and had my doubts as to whether this 1949 title should be kept.<br/><br/>It should.<br/><br/>I haven't decided on a reader for it yet, because the first half of the book is a lyrical character study of a drifter who arrives at a farm in Wyoming in 1889. While the family is happy, trouble is brewing. The father hires Shane to help with the work, and ends up getting more help than he bargains from the enigmatic stranger whose every move whispers "danger".<br/><br/>Onc</span>
  <span id="freeText3827886451365485783" style="display:none">Not a fan of westerns, never seen the movie with Alan Ladd, and had my doubts as to whether this 1949 title should be kept.<br /><br />It should.<br /><br />I haven't decided on a reader for it yet, because the first half of the book is a lyrical character study of a drifter who arrives at a farm in Wyoming in 1889. While the family is happy, trouble is brewing. The father hires Shane to help with the work, and ends up getting more help than he bargains from the enigmatic stranger whose every move whispers "danger".<br /><br />Once we find out that the evil rancher Fletcher is trying to take over all the small farms in the area using Wild West tactics (Won't sell your land? Fine. We'll just shoot and kill you.), we see how useful Shane is. Told from the point of view of the young son, this is more a look into what it takes to "be a man" and stand up for what is right than it is a shoot-em-up western, although the action picks up halfway through the book and there are a lot of bar room brawls and gun battles.<br /><br />I have to see the movie now, and someone must read this. It is a difficult book, though-- the father and Shane are both trying to do what is best for the family, even though it might be hard for them personally. I found Shane's sacrifice a touching change from the modern depiction of family interactions. As much a product of the 1950s as the 1890s, Shane's character is a vanished piece of Americana worth preserving.</span>
  <a data-text-id="3827886451365485783" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

              <span id="reviewTextContainer136690250" class="readable"

<span id="freeTextContainer9093808318933954926"><br/>A simple story of good and evil in a Wild West setting. I first read *Shane* 20 years ago while doing a week-long substitute teaching assignment in a junior high school English class, and expected to hate it. I ended up loving it and have reread it twice since then. It's one of those small books that has pretty much everything: An unforgettable title character, a thought-provoking plot, several exciting action scenes, and an ending that will break your heart. Put down that bloated yet hollow pos</span>
  <span id="freeText9093808318933954926" style="display:none"><br />A simple story of good and evil in a Wild West setting. I first read *Shane* 20 years ago while doing a week-long substitute teaching assignment in a junior high school English class, and expected to hate it. I ended up loving it and have reread it twice since then. It's one of those small books that has pretty much everything: An unforgettable title character, a thought-provoking plot, several exciting action scenes, and an ending that will break your heart. Put down that bloated yet hollow postmodern "literary fiction" that you're reading at the moment, and give *Shane* a try -- you won't regret it. </span>
  <a data-text-id="9093808318933954926" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Karen" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Karen" href="/user/show/4887506-karen">Karen</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Mass Market Paperback 9780553240764" href="/book/show/2766994-shane">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4887506-karen?shelf=christmas-in-july">christmas-in-july</a>,

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2897569571" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16254915021164248328">This small little book packed a punch! I haven't read a western book in a long time... but now that I think about it, I don't think I have ever read a western... or maybe I have and just can't remember. Either way, I loved this book! There were many parts I reread just to ponder over what message Schaefer was trying to convey. He imparts wisdom, wise advice, and the character of a man throughout this book. One of my favorite quotes is when Shane is telling Bob that a gun is just a tool:<br/>"Listen B</span>
  <span id="freeText16254915021164248328" style="display:none">This small little book packed a punch! I haven't read a western book in a long time... but now that I think about it, I don't think I have ever read a western... or maybe I have and just can't remember. Either way, I loved this book! There were many parts I reread just to ponder over what message Schaefer was trying to convey. He imparts wisdom, wise advice, and the character of a man throughout this book. One of my favorite quotes is when Shane is telling Bob that a gun is just a tool:<br />"Listen Bob. A gun is just a tool. No better and no worse than any other tool, a shovel-or an ax or a saddle or stove or anything. Think of it always that way. A gun is as good-and as bad-as the man who carries it. Remember that."<br />Wish more people felt this way. <br />Now I want to see the movie based on this book again. It's been awhile since I've seen it.<br />Special thanks to LibraryThings Member's Review Program for this free book and the opportunity to read and review it.</span>
  <a data-text-id="16254915021164248328" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_2397430070" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2397430070" />
    <a title="Bob Mayer" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/4460714-bob-mayer"><img alt="Bob Mayer" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1597686468p2/4460714.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2397430070?book_show_action=true">May 20, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Bob Mayer" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Bob Mayer" href="/user/show/4460714-bob-mayer">Bob Mayer</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2397430070" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9795367099088233517">A classic tale. I always preferred the gunman on the book cover over the buckskins in the movie. Doesn't seem like a gun slinger would be wearing buckskins, but maybe that's just me.<br/><br/>A lot of subtext between Shane and the mother-- did he really walk/ride away because you can't live with a killing or because of her?<br/><br/>Ultimately, this book touches on our fascination with guns, a timely topic. It falls in with the mantra that the only way to stop a bad guy with a gun is a good guy with a gun. While th</span>
  <span id="freeText9795367099088233517" style="display:none">A classic tale. I always preferred the gunman on the book cover over the buckskins in the movie. Doesn't seem like a gun slinger would be wearing buckskins, but maybe that's just me.<br /><br />A lot of subtext between Shane and the mother-- did he really walk/ride away because you can't live with a killing or because of her?<br /><br />Ultimately, this book touches on our fascination with guns, a timely topic. It falls in with the mantra that the only way to stop a bad guy with a gun is a good guy with a gun. While that might have been on the frontier, it's not something that is really relevant to our current world. But the mythos persists.</span>
  <a data-text-id="9795367099088233517" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>


      <div class="reviewFooter uitext buttons">

<div id="review_3616170982" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">

    <a title="Sharone Powell" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/8987415-sharone-powell"><img alt="Sharone Powell" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1335192704p2/8987415.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Sharone Powell" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Sharone Powell" href="/user/show/8987415-sharone-powell">Sharone Powell</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3616170982" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15131380208557239970">A wonderful book about what it means to be a real man. <br/><br/>(Spoiler Alert!!!)<br/>Alas, Shane's figure is a tragic one. He can recognize the greatness in Joe Starrett and what he has with his family so much so that he defends it with his life, but he himself can never have it. How sad.<br/>Also, he rides away with a bloody gut with no treatment or shelter in sight, leaving us wondering if he's going to survive. I think the point Jack Schaefer was making was that Shane was the last - and the finest - of a dyin</span>
  <span id="freeText15131380208557239970" style="display:none">A wonderful book about what it means to be a real man. <br /><br />(Spoiler Alert!!!)<br />Alas, Shane's figure is a tragic one. He can recognize the greatness in Joe Starrett and what he has with his family so much so that he defends it with his life, but he himself can never have it. How sad.<br />Also, he rides away with a bloody gut with no treatment or shelter in sight, leaving us wondering if he's going to survive. I think the point Jack Schaefer was making was that Shane was the last - and the finest - of a dying breed. </span>

<div class=" clearFloats bigBox"><div class="h2Container gradientHeaderContainer"><h2 class="brownBackground">Readers also enjoyed</h2></div><div class="bigBoxBody"><div class="bigBoxContent containerWithHeaderContent"><div class='bookCarousel'>
<div class='carouselRow' style='width: 3600px'>

<script type="text/javascript" charset="utf-8">
//<![CDATA[  

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('koex8K3s8deUdbE65ihbmXFAcQBWS6Tf65dyVcQLI+K3TJan0Qc1S9x7VtxBrYjEnsX2sDZayqYPSN1wO/96dQ==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  function refreshGroupBox(group_id, book_id) {
    new Ajax.Updater('addGroupBooks' + book_id + '', '/group/add_book_box', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){refreshGroupBoxComplete(request, book_id);}, parameters:'id=' + group_id + '&book_id=' + book_id + '&refresh=true' + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('eykdjXz5vYNgc7dBgLHVssbJPLc24lx9p8Cmr3Ve9fxe4jraABJ5Hyh9UKcnNAbvKUy7B1bzMgRDHwmKiqqsaw==')})
  }
//]]>
</script>

First published in the 1950s, at the height of the McCarthy era, <\/span>\n  <span id=\"freeText10627068813502766257\" style=\"display:none\">Oakley Hall\'s legendary Warlock revisits and reworks the traditional conventions of the Western to present a raw, funny, hypnotic, ultimately devastating picture of American unreality. First published in the 1950s, at the height of the McCarthy era, Warlock is not only one of the most original and entertaining of modern American novels but a lasting contribution to American fiction.\n\n&quot;Tombstone, Arizona, during the 1880\'s is, in ways, our national Camelot: a never-never land where American virtues are embodied in the Earps, and the opposite evils in the Clanton gang; where the confrontation at the OK Corral takes on some of the dry purity of the Arthurian joust. Oakley Hall, in his very fine novel Warlock has restored to the myth of Tombstone its full, mortal, blooded humanity. Wyatt Earp is transmogrified into a gunfighter named Blaisdell who . . . is summoned to the embattled town of Warlock by a committee of nervous citizens expressly to be a hero, but finds that he cannot, at last, live up to his image; that there is a flaw not only in him, but also, we feel, in the entire set of assumptions that have allowed the image to exist. . . . Before the agonized epic of Warlock is over with--the rebellion of the proto-Wobblies working in the mines, the struggling for political control of the area, the gunfighting, mob violence, the personal crises of those in power--the collective awareness that is Warlock must face its own inescapable Horror: that what is called society, with its law and order, is as frail, as precarious, as flesh and can be snuffed out and assimilated back into the desert as easily as a corpse can. It is the deep sensitivity to abysses that makes Warlock one of our best American novels. For we are a nation that can, many of us, toss with all aplomb our candy wrapper into the Grand Canyon itself, snap a color shot and drive away; and we need voices like Oakley Hall\'s to remind us how far that piece of paper, still fluttering brightly behind us, has to fall.&quot; --Thomas Pynchon<\/span>\n  <a data-text-id=\"10627068813502766257\" href=\"#\" onclick=\"swapContent(\$(this));;       <div id="aboutAuthor" class=" clearFloats bigBox"><div class="h2Container gradientHeaderContainer"><h2 class="brownBackground"><a href="/author/show/150701.Jack_Schaefer">About Jack Schaefer</a></h2></div><div class="bigBoxBody"><div class="bigBoxContent containerWithHeaderContent">
          <div class='bookAuthorProfile'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__topContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__photoContainer'>
<a href="/author/show/150701.Jack_Schaefer"><div class='bookAuthorProfile__photo' style='background-image: url(https://images.gr-assets.com/authors/1342979492p3/150701.jpg);'></div>

<div class='bookAuthorProfile__widgetContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__name'>
<a href="/author/show/150701.Jack_Schaefer">Jack Schaefer
</a></div>

<div class='bookAuthorProfile__about'>

<span id="freeTextContainer14470232930278327017">Schaefer was born in Cleveland, Ohio, the son of an attorney. He graduated from Oberlin College in 1929 with a major in English. He attended graduate school at Columbia University from 1929-30, but left without completing his Master of Arts degree. He then went to work for the United Press. In his long career as a journalist, he would hold editorial positions at many eastern publications.<br/> <br/>Schaefer</span>
  <span id="freeText14470232930278327017" style="display:none">Schaefer was born in Cleveland, Ohio, the son of an attorney. He graduated from Oberlin College in 1929 with a major in English. He attended graduate school at Columbia University from 1929-30, but left without completing his Master of Arts degree. He then went to work for the United Press. In his long career as a journalist, he would hold editorial positions at many eastern publications.<br /> <br />Schaefer's first success as a novelist came in 1949 with his memorable novel <i>Shane</i>, set in Wyoming. Few realized that Schaefer himself had never been anywhere near the west. Nevertheless, he continued writing successful westerns, selling his home in Connecticut and moving to Santa Fe, New Mexico in 1955.<br /> <br />In 1975 Schaefer received the Western Literature Association's Distinguished Achievement award.<br /> <br />He died of heart failure in Santa Fe in 1991. Schaefer was married twice, his second wife moving to Santa Fe with him.<br /> <br />Schaefer's novel <i>Monte Walsh</i> was made into a movie in 1970, with Lee Marvin in the title role, and again in 2003 as a TV movie starring Tom Selleck. Shane was also made into a movie and a series.<br /><br />from Wikipedia: <br /><a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Jack_Schaefer">http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Jack_Sch...</a></span>
  <a data-text-id="14470232930278327017" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

</div>

He was back somewhere along the dark trail of the past.
